<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Medal of Honor</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">

<p>The <b>Medal of Honor</b> is the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States of America's</a> highest <a href="/wiki/Awards_and_decorations_of_the_United_States_Armed_Forces" title="Awards and decorations of the United States Armed Forces">military honor</a>, awarded for personal acts of <a href="/wiki/Courage" title="Courage">valor</a> above and beyond the call of duty. The medal is awarded by the <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">President of the United States</a> in the name of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">U.S. Congress</a> to U.S. military personnel only. There are three versions of the medal, one for the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Army" title="United States Army">Army</a>, one for the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy" title="United States Navy">Navy</a>, and one for the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Air_Force" title="United States Air Force">Air Force</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> Personnel of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Marine_Corps" title="United States Marine Corps">Marine Corps</a> and <a href="/wiki/United_States_Coast_Guard" title="United States Coast Guard">Coast Guard</a> receive the Navy version. US awards including the Medal of Honor do not have <a href="/wiki/Post-nominal_letters" title="Post-nominal letters">post-nominal titles</a> and while there is no official abbreviation, the most common abbreviations are "MOH" and "MH".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Medal of Honor was created as a Navy version in 1861 named the "Medal of Valor",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-defense.gov_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-defense.gov-7">[7]</a></sup> and an Army version of the medal named the "Medal of Honor" was established in 1862 to give recognition to men who distinguished themselves "conspicuously by gallantry and intrepidity" in combat with an enemy of the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> Because the medal is presented "in the name of Congress", it is often referred to as the "Congressional Medal of Honor". However, the official name is "Medal of Honor", which began with the U.S. Army's version.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-regulations_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-regulations-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> Within <a href="/wiki/United_States_Code" title="United States Code">United States Code</a> the medal is referred to as the "Medal of Honor",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> and less frequently as "Congressional Medal of Honor".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Medal of Honor is usually presented by the President in a formal ceremony at the <a href="/wiki/White_House" title="White House">White House</a>, intended to represent the gratitude of the American people, with posthumous presentations made to the primary next of kin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-posthumousQuantification_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-posthumousQuantification-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> According to the Medal of Honor Historical Society of the United States, there have been 3,515 Medals of Honor awarded to the nation's soldiers, sailors, airmen, Marines, and Coast Guardsmen since the decoration's creation, with just less than half of them awarded for actions during the four years of the American Civil War.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AMOHWStat_4-3"><a href="#cite_note-AMOHWStat-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1990, Congress designated March 25 annually as "National Medal of Honor Day".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> Due to its prestige and status, the Medal of Honor is afforded special protection under U.S. law against any unauthorized adornment, sale, or manufacture, which includes any associated ribbon or badge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Medal_of_Honor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>1780: The <a href="/wiki/Fidelity_Medallion" title="Fidelity Medallion">Fidelity Medallion</a> was a small medal worn on a chain around the neck, similar to a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Religious" title="Religious">religious</a> medal, that was awarded only to three <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Militiamen" title="Militiamen">militiamen</a> from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_York_state" title="New York state">New York state</a>, for the capture of <a href="/wiki/John_Andr%C3%A9" title="John André">John André</a>, a <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">British</a> officer and spy connected directly to General <a href="/wiki/Benedict_Arnold" title="Benedict Arnold">Benedict Arnold</a> (American and British general-1780) during the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolutionary_War" title="American Revolutionary War">American Revolutionary War</a> (1775–1783). The capture saved the fort of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/West_Point" title="West Point">West Point</a> from the British Army.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>1782: <a href="/wiki/Badge_of_Military_Merit" title="Badge of Military Merit">Badge of Military Merit</a>: The first formal system for rewarding acts of individual gallantry by American soldiers was established by <a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">George Washington</a> when he issued a field order on August 7, 1782, for a Badge of Military Merit to recognize those members of the <a href="/wiki/Continental_Army" title="Continental Army">Continental Army</a> who performed "any singular meritorious action". This decoration is America's first combat decoration and was preceded only by the Fidelity Medallion, the Congressional medal for Henry Lee awarded in September 1779 in recognition of his attack on the British at Paulus Hook, the Congressional medal for General Horatio Gates awarded in November 1777 in recognition of his victory over the British at Saratoga, and the Congressional medal for George Washington awarded in March 1776.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-regulations_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-regulations-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-merit_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-merit-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> Although the Badge of Military Merit fell into disuse after the American Revolutionary War, the concept of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Military_award" title="Military award">military award</a> for individual gallantry by members of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Armed_Forces" title="U.S. Armed Forces">U.S. Armed Forces</a> had been established.</p>
<p>1847: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Certificate_of_Merit" title="Certificate of Merit">Certificate of Merit</a>: After the outbreak of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mexican-American_War" title="Mexican-American War">Mexican-American War</a> (1846–1848) a <a href="/wiki/Certificate_of_Merit_Medal" title="Certificate of Merit Medal">Certificate of Merit</a> (Meritorious Service Citation Certificate) was established by Act of Congress on March 3, 1847 "to any private soldier who had distinguished himself by gallantry performed in the presence of the enemy". 539 Certificates were approved for this period. The certificate was discontinued and reintroduced in 1876 effective from June 22, 1874 to February 10, 1892 when it was awarded for extraordinary gallantry by private soldiers in the presence of the enemy. From February 11, 1892 through July 9, 1918 (Certificate of Merit disestablished) it could be awarded to members of the Army for distinguished service in combat or noncombat; from January 11, 1905 through July 9, 1918 the certificate was granted medal status as the <a href="/wiki/Certificate_of_Merit_Medal" title="Certificate of Merit Medal">Certificate of Merit Medal</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zabecki2008_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zabecki2008-18">[18]</a></sup> (first awarded to a soldier who was awarded the Certificate of Merit for combat action on August 13, 1898). This medal was later replaced by the <a href="/wiki/Distinguished_Service_Medal_(U.S._Army)" title="Distinguished Service Medal (U.S. Army)">Army Distinguished Service Medal</a> which was established on January 2, 1918 (the <a href="/wiki/Distinguished_Service_Medal_(United_States_Navy)" title="Distinguished Service Medal (United States Navy)">Navy Distinguished Service Medal</a> was established in 1919). Those Army members who held the Distinguished Service Medal in place of the Certificate of Merit could apply for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Distinguished_Service_Cross_(United_States_Army)" title="Distinguished Service Cross (United States Army)">Army Distinguished Service Cross</a> (established 1918) effective March 5, 1934.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medal_of_Valor">Medal of Valor</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Medal_of_Honor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Medal of Valor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:160px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ortega_meda_of_Honor.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="200" data-file-width="158" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/19/Ortega_meda_of_Honor.jpg" width="158"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ortega_meda_of_Honor.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Medal of Honor (without the suspension ribbon) awarded to <a href="/wiki/Seaman" title="Seaman">Seaman</a> <a href="/wiki/John_Ortega" title="John Ortega">John Ortega</a> in 1864 (back view of medal).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>There were no military awards or medals at the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">Civil War</a> (1861–1865) except for the Certificate of Merit which was awarded for the Mexican-American War. In the fall of 1861, a proposal for a battlefield decoration for <a href="/wiki/Courage" title="Courage">valor</a> was submitted to <a href="/wiki/Winfield_Scott" title="Winfield Scott">Winfield Scott</a>, the general-in-chief of the army, by Lt. Colonel <a href="/wiki/Edward_D._Townsend" title="Edward D. Townsend">Edward D. Townsend</a>, an assistant adjutant at the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_War" title="United States Department of War">War Department</a> and Scott's chief of staff. Scott however, was strictly against medals being awarded which was the European tradition. After Scott retired in October 1861, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Secretary_of_the_Navy" title="Secretary of the Navy">Secretary of the Navy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gideon_Welles" title="Gideon Welles">Gideon Welles</a>, adopted the idea of a decoration to recognize and honor distinguished naval service. On December 9, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Senator" title="U.S. Senator">U.S. Senator</a> (<a href="/wiki/Iowa" title="Iowa">Iowa</a>) <a href="/wiki/James_W._Grimes" title="James W. Grimes">James W. Grimes</a>, Chairman on the Committee on Naval Affairs,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> proposed Public Resolution Number 82<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> (Bill 82: 37th Congress, Second Session, 12 Stat. 329) "to promote the efficiency of the Navy" which included a provision for a Navy Medal of Valor<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-defense.gov_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-defense.gov-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> which was signed into law by President <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Lincoln" title="Abraham Lincoln">Abraham Lincoln</a> on December 21, 1861 (Medal of Honor had been established for the Navy), "to be bestowed upon such <a href="/wiki/Petty_officer" title="Petty officer">petty officers</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seamen" title="Seamen">seamen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Landsman_(rank)" title="Landsman (rank)">landsmen</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Marines" title="Marines">marines</a> as shall most distinguish themselves by their gallantry and other seamen-like qualities during the present war."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Secretary Wells directed the <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia_Mint" title="Philadelphia Mint">Philadelphia Mint</a> to design the new military decoration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-valorhonor_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-valorhonor-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MWxviii_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-MWxviii-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-typessecnav_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-typessecnav-25">[25]</a></sup> On May 15, 1862, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_Navy_Department" title="United States Navy Department">United States Navy Department</a> ordered 175 medals ($1.85 each) with the words "Personal Valor" on the back from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Mint" title="U.S. Mint">U.S. Mint</a> in <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia" title="Philadelphia">Philadelphia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medal_of_Honor">Medal of Honor</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Medal_of_Honor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Medal of Honor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Senator <a href="/wiki/Henry_Wilson" title="Henry Wilson">Henry Wilson</a>, the chairman of the Senate Committee on Military Affairs, introduced a resolution on February 15, 1862 for an Army Medal of Honor. The resolution (37th Congress, Second Session, 12 Stat. 623) was approved by Congress and signed into law on July 12, 1862 ("Medals of Honor" were established for enlisted men of the Army). This measure provided for awarding a medal of honor "to such non-commissioned officers and privates as shall most distinguish themselves by their gallantry in action and other soldier-like qualities during the present insurrection." During the war, Townsend would have some medals delivered to some recipients with a letter requesting acknowledgement of the "Medal of Honor". The letter written and signed by Townsend on behalf of the Secretary of War, stated that the resolution was "to provide for the presentation of medals of honor to the enlisted men of the army and volunteer forces who have distinguished or may distinguish themselves in battle during the present rebellion."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> By mid-November the War Department contracted with Philadelphia <a href="/wiki/Silversmith" title="Silversmith">silversmith</a> William Wilson and Son, who had been responsible for the Navy design, to prepare 2,000 Army medals ($2.00 each) to be cast at the mint.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> The Army version had "The Congress to" written on the back of the medal. Both versions were made of <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a> and coated with <a href="/wiki/Bronze" title="Bronze">bronze</a>, which "gave them a reddish tint".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p>1863: Congress made the Medal of Honor a permanent decoration. On March 3, Medals of Honor were authorized for officers of the Army<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> (37th Congress, Third Session, 12 Stat. 751). The Secretary of War first presented the Medal of Honor to six Union Army volunteers on March 25, 1863 in his office.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<p>1890: On April 23, the Medal of Honor Legion is established in Washington, D.C.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CMOHShistory_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-CMOHShistory-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<p>1896: The ribbon of the Army version Medal of Honor was redesigned with all stripes being vertical.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RHargis2012_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-RHargis2012-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<p>1904: The planchet of the Army version of the Medal of Honor was redesigned by General <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/George_Lewis_Gillespie" title="George Lewis Gillespie">George Lewis Gillespie</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RHargis2012_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-RHargis2012-38">[38]</a></sup> The purpose of the redesign was to help distinguish the Medal of Honor from other medals,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> including a medal issued by the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Army_of_the_Republic" title="Grand Army of the Republic">Grand Army of the Republic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<p>1915: On March 3, Navy, Marine Corps, and Coast Guard officers became eligible for the Medal of Honor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CMOHShistory_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-CMOHShistory-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>1963: A separate Coast Guard medal was authorized in 1963, but not yet designed or awarded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-navyfaq_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-navyfaq-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<p>1965: A separate design for a version of the medal for the U.S. Air Force was created in 1956, authorized in 1960, and officially adopted on April 14, 1965. Previously, members of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Army_Air_Corps" title="U.S. Army Air Corps">U.S. Army Air Corps</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Army_Air_Forces" title="U.S. Army Air Forces">U.S. Army Air Forces</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Air_Force" title="U.S. Air Force">U.S. Air Force</a> received the Army version of the medal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MWxxvi_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-MWxxvi-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Appearance">Appearance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Medal_of_Honor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Appearance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>There are three versions of the Medal of Honor, one for each of the <a href="/wiki/Organizational_structure_of_the_United_States_Department_of_Defense" title="Organizational structure of the United States Department of Defense">military departments</a> of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Defense" title="United States Department of Defense">Department of Defense</a>: Army, Navy, and Air Force. Members of the Marine Corps and Coast Guard are eligible to receive the Navy version. Each is constructed differently and the components are made from gilding metals and red <a href="/wiki/Brass" title="Brass">brass</a> alloys with some gold plating, enamel, and bronze pieces. The United States Congress considered a bill in 2004 which would require the Medal of Honor to be made with 90% gold, the same composition as the lesser-known <a href="/wiki/Congressional_Gold_Medal" title="Congressional Gold Medal">Congressional Gold Medal</a>, but the measure was dropped.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ABC-GoldorBrass_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-ABC-GoldorBrass-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Army_Medal_of_Honor">Army Medal of Honor</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Medal_of_Honor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Army Medal of Honor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:77px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Army_Medal_of_Honor.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="205" data-file-width="154" height="100" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Army_Medal_of_Honor.jpg/75px-Army_Medal_of_Honor.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Army_Medal_of_Honor.jpg/113px-Army_Medal_of_Honor.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Army_Medal_of_Honor.jpg/150px-Army_Medal_of_Honor.jpg 2x" width="75"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Army_Medal_of_Honor.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Army version</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Army version is described by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Army_Institute_of_Heraldry" title="United States Army Institute of Heraldry">Institute of Heraldry</a> as "a gold five pointed star, each point tipped with trefoils, <span class="frac nowrap">1<span class="visualhide"> </span><sup>1</sup>⁄<sub>2</sub></span> inches [3.8 cm] wide, surrounded by a green laurel wreath and suspended from a gold bar inscribed <i>VALOR</i>, surmounted by an eagle. In the center of the star, <a href="/wiki/Minerva" title="Minerva">Minerva</a>'s head surrounded by the words <i>UNITED STATES OF AMERICA</i>. On each ray of the star is a green oak leaf. On the reverse is a bar engraved <i>THE CONGRESS TO</i> with a space for engraving the name of the recipient."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TIOH-MOH_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-TIOH-MOH-46">[46]</a></sup> The pendant and suspension bar are made of gilding metal, with the eye, jump rings, and suspension ring made of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Red_brass" title="Red brass">red brass</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MIL-DTL-3943.2F1F_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-MIL-DTL-3943.2F1F-47">[47]</a></sup> The finish on the pendant and suspension bar is hard enameled, gold plated, and rose gold plated, with polished highlights.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MIL-DTL-3943.2F1F_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-MIL-DTL-3943.2F1F-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Navy.2C_Marine.2C_and_Coast_Guard_Medal_of_Honor">Navy, Marine, and Coast Guard Medal of Honor</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Medal_of_Honor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Navy, Marine, and Coast Guard Medal of Honor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:77px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NavyMedalofHonor.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="305" data-file-width="225" height="102" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/NavyMedalofHonor.jpg/75px-NavyMedalofHonor.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/NavyMedalofHonor.jpg/113px-NavyMedalofHonor.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/NavyMedalofHonor.jpg/150px-NavyMedalofHonor.jpg 2x" width="75"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NavyMedalofHonor.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Navy version</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Navy version is described as "a five-pointed bronze star, tipped with trefoils containing a crown of laurel and oak. In the center is Minerva, personifying the United States, standing with left hand resting on fasces and right hand holding a shield blazoned with the shield from the coat of arms of the United States. She repulses Discord, represented by snakes. The medal is suspended from the flukes of an anchor."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TIOH-MOH_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-TIOH-MOH-46">[46]</a></sup> It is made of solid red brass, oxidized and buffed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MIL-DTL-3943.2F2H_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-MIL-DTL-3943.2F2H-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Air_Force_Medal_of_Honor">Air Force Medal of Honor</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Medal_of_Honor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Air Force Medal of Honor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:77px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:AirForceMedalofHonor.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="201" data-file-width="131" height="115" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/AirForceMedalofHonor.jpg/75px-AirForceMedalofHonor.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/AirForceMedalofHonor.jpg/113px-AirForceMedalofHonor.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e1/AirForceMedalofHonor.jpg 2x" width="75"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:AirForceMedalofHonor.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Air Force version</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Air Force version is described as "within a wreath of green laurel, a gold five-pointed star, one point down, tipped with trefoils and each point containing a crown of laurel and oak on a green background. Centered on the star, an annulet of 34 stars is a representation of the head of the <a href="/wiki/Libertas" title="Libertas">Statue of Liberty</a>. The star is suspended from a bar inscribed with the word <i>VALOR</i> above an adaptation of the thunderbolt from the Air Force Coat of Arms."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TIOH-MOH_46-2"><a href="#cite_note-TIOH-MOH-46">[46]</a></sup> The pendant is made of gilding metal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MIL-DTL-3943.2F3G_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-MIL-DTL-3943.2F3G-49">[49]</a></sup> The connecting bar, hinge, and pin are made of bronze.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MIL-DTL-3943.2F3G_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-MIL-DTL-3943.2F3G-49">[49]</a></sup> The finish on the pendant and suspension bar is hard enameled, gold plated, and rose gold plated, with buffed relief.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MIL-DTL-3943.2F3G_49-2"><a href="#cite_note-MIL-DTL-3943.2F3G-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Neck_ribbon.2C_service_ribbon.2C_lapel_button.2C_and_.22V.22_device">Neck ribbon, service ribbon, lapel button, and "V" device</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Medal_of_Honor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title='Edit section: Neck ribbon, service ribbon, lapel button, and "V" device'>edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:92px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Medal_of_Honor_ribbon.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="30" data-file-width="106" height="25" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Medal_of_Honor_ribbon.svg/90px-Medal_of_Honor_ribbon.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Medal_of_Honor_ribbon.svg/135px-Medal_of_Honor_ribbon.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Medal_of_Honor_ribbon.svg/180px-Medal_of_Honor_ribbon.svg.png 2x" width="90"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">

<a href="/wiki/Service_ribbon" title="Service ribbon">Service ribbon</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:32px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Moh_rosette.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="69" data-file-width="67" height="31" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Moh_rosette.gif/30px-Moh_rosette.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Moh_rosette.gif/45px-Moh_rosette.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Moh_rosette.gif/60px-Moh_rosette.gif 2x" width="30"/></a>

</div>

<div class="thumbinner" style="width:22px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Valor_device.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2467" data-file-width="2400" height="21" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Valor_device.jpg/20px-Valor_device.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Valor_device.jpg/30px-Valor_device.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Valor_device.jpg/40px-Valor_device.jpg 2x" width="20"/></a>

</div>
</div>
<p>Since 1944, the Medal of Honor has been attached to a light blue<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> colored <a href="/wiki/Moir%C3%A9_pattern" title="Moiré pattern">moiré</a> silk <a href="/wiki/Neck_order" title="Neck order">Neck ribbon</a> that is <span class="frac nowrap">1<span class="visualhide"> </span><sup>3</sup>⁄<sub>16</sub></span> in (30 mm) in width and <span class="frac nowrap">21<span class="visualhide"> </span><sup>3</sup>⁄<sub>4</sub></span> in (550 mm) in length.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-regulations_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-regulations-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-versions_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-versions-51">[51]</a></sup> The center of the ribbon displays <a href="/wiki/Thirteen_Colonies" title="Thirteen Colonies">thirteen white stars</a> in the form of three <a href="/wiki/Chevron_(insignia)" title="Chevron (insignia)">chevron</a>. Both the top and middle chevrons are made up of 5 stars, with the bottom chevron made of 3 stars. The Medal of Honor is one of only two United States military awards suspended from a neck ribbon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> The other is the Commander's Degree of the <a href="/wiki/Legion_of_Merit" title="Legion of Merit">Legion of Merit</a>, and is usually awarded to individuals serving foreign governments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-neckorderLOM_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-neckorderLOM-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<p>On May 2, 1896, Congress authorized a "ribbon to be worn with the medal and [a] <a href="/wiki/Rosette_(decoration)" title="Rosette (decoration)">rosette</a> or <a href="/wiki/Knot" title="Knot">knot</a> to be worn in lieu of the medal."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-typessecnav_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-typessecnav-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TIOH-MOH_46-3"><a href="#cite_note-TIOH-MOH-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Service_ribbon" title="Service ribbon">service ribbon</a> is light blue with five white stars in the form of an "M".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TIOH-MOH_46-4"><a href="#cite_note-TIOH-MOH-46">[46]</a></sup> It is placed first in the top position in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Awards_and_decorations_of_the_United_States_military#Order_of_precedence" title="Awards and decorations of the United States military">order of precedence</a> and is worn for situations other than full-dress military uniform.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TIOH-MOH_46-5"><a href="#cite_note-TIOH-MOH-46">[46]</a></sup> The lapel button is a <span class="frac nowrap"><sup>1</sup>⁄<sub>2</sub></span>-inch (13 mm), six-sided light blue <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bowknot" title="Bowknot">bowknot</a> rosette with thirteen white stars and may be worn on appropriate civilian clothing on the left lapel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TIOH-MOH_46-6"><a href="#cite_note-TIOH-MOH-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2011, Department of Defense instructions were amended to read "for each succeeding act that would otherwise justify award of the Medal of Honor, the individual receiving the subsequent award is authorized to wear an additional Medal of Honor ribbon and/or a <a href="/wiki/%22V%22_Device" title='"V" Device'>'V' device</a> on the Medal of Honor suspension ribbon" (the "V" device is a <span class="frac nowrap"><sup>1</sup>⁄<sub>4</sub></span>-inch (6.4 mm) high bronze miniature letter "V" with serifs that denotes valor). The Medal of Honor was the only decoration authorized the use of the "V" device to designate subsequent awards in such fashion. Nineteen individuals, all now deceased, were double Medal of Honor recipients.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-double_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-double-56">[56]</a></sup> This was discontinued in July 2014, and changed to read "A separate MOH is presented to an individual for each succeeding act that justified award."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> As of 2014, no devices are authorized for the Medal of Honor.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_versions">Historical versions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Medal_of_Honor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Historical versions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tiffany_Cross_Medal_of_Honor" title="Tiffany Cross Medal of Honor">Tiffany Cross Medal of Honor</a></div>
<p>The Medal of Honor has evolved in appearance over time. The upside-down star design of the Navy version's pendant adopted in early 1862 has not changed since its inception. The Army 1862 version followed and was identical to the Navy version except an eagle perched atop cannons was used instead of an anchor to connect the pendant to the suspension ribbon. In 1896, the Army version changed the ribbon's design and colors due to misuse and imitation by nonmilitary organizations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TIOH-MOH_46-7"><a href="#cite_note-TIOH-MOH-46">[46]</a></sup> In 1904, the Army "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/George_Lewis_Gillespie,_Jr." title="George Lewis Gillespie, Jr.">Gillespie</a>" version introduced a smaller redesigned star and the ribbon was changed to the light blue pattern with white stars seen today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TIOH-MOH_46-8"><a href="#cite_note-TIOH-MOH-46">[46]</a></sup> In 1913, the Navy version adopted the same ribbon pattern.</p>
<p>After World War I, the Navy decided to separate the Medal of Honor into two versions, one for combat and one for non-combat. The original upside-down star was designated as the non-combat version and a new pattern of the medal pendant, in cross form, was designed by the <a href="/wiki/Tiffany_%26_Co." title="Tiffany &amp; Co.">Tiffany Company</a> in 1919. It was to be presented to a sailor or Marine who "in action involving actual conflict with the enemy, distinguish[es] himself conspicuously by gallantry and intrepidity at the risk of his life above and beyond the call of duty"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> Despite the "actual conflict" guidelines—the <i>Tiffany Cross</i> was awarded to Navy CDR (later RADM) <a href="/wiki/Richard_E._Byrd" title="Richard E. Byrd">Richard E. Byrd</a> and <a href="/wiki/Floyd_Bennett" title="Floyd Bennett">Floyd Bennett</a> for arctic exploration. The <i>Tiffany Cross</i> itself was not popular. In 1942, the Navy returned to using only the original 1862 inverted 5-point star design, and ceased issuing the award for non-combat action.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1944, the suspension ribbons for both the Army and Navy version were replaced with the now familiar neck ribbon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TIOH-MOH_46-9"><a href="#cite_note-TIOH-MOH-46">[46]</a></sup> When the Air Force version was designed in 1956, it incorporated similar elements and design from the Army version. It used a larger star with the Statue of Liberty image in place of Minerva on the medal and changed the connecting device from an eagle to an heraldic thunderbolt flanked with wings as found on the service seal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Flag">Flag</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Medal_of_Honor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Flag">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Medal_of_Honor_Flag_with_Gold_Fringe.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="420" data-file-width="523" height="177" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Medal_of_Honor_Flag_with_Gold_Fringe.svg/220px-Medal_of_Honor_Flag_with_Gold_Fringe.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Medal_of_Honor_Flag_with_Gold_Fringe.svg/330px-Medal_of_Honor_Flag_with_Gold_Fringe.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Medal_of_Honor_Flag_with_Gold_Fringe.svg/440px-Medal_of_Honor_Flag_with_Gold_Fringe.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Medal_of_Honor_Flag_with_Gold_Fringe.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Medal of Honor Flag</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On October 23, 2002, <a class="external text" href="http://legislink.org/us/pl-107-248" rel="nofollow">Pub.L. 107–248</a> was enacted, modifying <a href="/wiki/Title_36_of_the_United_States_Code" title="Title 36 of the United States Code">36 U.S.C.</a> <a class="external text" href="https://www.law.cornell.edu/uscode/text/36/903" rel="nofollow">§ 903</a>, authorizing a Medal of Honor flag to be presented to recipients of the decoration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-flaglaw_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-flaglaw-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<p>The flag was based on a concept by retired <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Army_Special_Forces" title="U.S. Army Special Forces">Army Special Forces</a> First Sergeant Bill Kendall of <a href="/wiki/Jefferson,_Iowa" title="Jefferson, Iowa">Jefferson, Iowa</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kendall_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kendall-63">[63]</a></sup> who designed a flag to honor Medal of Honor recipient Captain <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Darrell_Lindsey" title="Darrell Lindsey">Darrell Lindsey</a>, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/B-26_Marauder" title="B-26 Marauder">B-26</a> pilot from Jefferson who was killed in World War II. Kendall's design of a light blue field emblazoned with 13 white five-pointed stars was nearly identical to that of Sarah LeClerc's of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Army_Institute_of_Heraldry" title="U.S. Army Institute of Heraldry">Institute of Heraldry</a>. LeClerc's design, ultimately accepted as the official flag, does not include the words "Medal of Honor" and is fringed in gold. The color of the field and the 13 white stars, arranged in the form of a three bar <a href="/wiki/Chevron_(insignia)" title="Chevron (insignia)">chevron</a>, consisting of two chevrons of five stars and one chevron of three stars,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-regulations_1-4"><a href="#cite_note-regulations-1">[1]</a></sup> emulate the suspension ribbon of the Medal of Honor. The flag has no set proportions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-flag_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-flag-64">[64]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>The first Medal of Honor recipient to receive the official Medal of Honor flag was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sergeant_First_Class" title="Sergeant First Class">Sergeant First Class</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paul_R._Smith" title="Paul R. Smith">Paul R. Smith</a>. The Medal of Honor with the flag was presented by President <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a> to his family during a ceremony at the <a href="/wiki/White_House" title="White House">White House</a> on April 4, 2005.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-smithflag_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-smithflag-65">[65]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<p>A special Medal of Honor Flag presentation ceremony was held for over 60 living Medal of Honor recipients on board the <a href="/wiki/USS_Constitution" title="USS Constitution">USS <i>Constitution</i></a> on September 30, 2006.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-flagpresent_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-flagpresent-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Presenting">Presenting</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Medal_of_Honor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Presenting">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Henry_Breault2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2854" data-file-width="3610" height="174" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Henry_Breault2.jpg/220px-Henry_Breault2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Henry_Breault2.jpg/330px-Henry_Breault2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Henry_Breault2.jpg/440px-Henry_Breault2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Henry_Breault2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
President <a href="/wiki/Calvin_Coolidge" title="Calvin Coolidge">Calvin Coolidge</a> bestowing the Medal of Honor upon <a href="/wiki/Henry_Breault" title="Henry Breault">Henry Breault</a>, March 8, 1924.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>There are two distinct protocols for awarding the Medal of Honor. The first and most common is nomination and approval through the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chain_of_command" title="Chain of command">chain of command</a> of the service member. The second method is nomination by a member of the U.S. Congress, generally at the request of a constituent. In both cases, if the proposal is outside the time limits for the recommendation, approval to waive the time limit requires a special <a href="/wiki/Act_of_Congress" title="Act of Congress">Act of Congress</a>. The Medal of Honor is presented by the President on behalf of, and in the name of, the Congress.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> Since 1980, nearly all Medal of Honor recipients or in the case of posthumous awards, the next of kin have been personally decorated by the Commander-in-Chief.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> Since 1941, more than half of the Medals of Honor have been awarded posthumously.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolution_of_criteria">Evolution of criteria</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Medal_of_Honor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Evolution of criteria">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li>19th century: Several months after President Abraham Lincoln signed Public Resolution 82 into law on December 21, 1861 for a Navy medal of honor, a similar resolution was passed in July 1862 for an Army version of the medal. Six <a href="/wiki/Union_Army" title="Union Army">Union Army</a> soldiers who <a href="/wiki/Great_Locomotive_Chase" title="Great Locomotive Chase">hijacked</a> a <a href="/wiki/Confederate_States_of_America" title="Confederate States of America">Confederate</a> <a href="/wiki/Locomotive" title="Locomotive">locomotive</a> named <a href="/wiki/The_General_(locomotive)" title="The General (locomotive)"><i>The General</i></a> in 1862, were the first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medal_of_Honor_recipients" title="Medal of Honor recipients">Medal of Honor recipients</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MWxvii_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-MWxvii-72">[72]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/James_J._Andrews" title="James J. Andrews">James J. Andrews</a>, a civilian, led the raid. He was caught and hanged as a Union spy, but was a civilian and not eligible to receive the medal. Many Medals of Honor awarded in the 19th century were associated with "saving the flag" (and country), not just for patriotic reasons, but because the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._flag" title="U.S. flag">U.S. flag</a> was a primary means of battlefield communication at the time. Because no other military decoration was authorized during the Civil War, some seemingly less exceptional and notable actions were recognized by a Medal of Honor during that conflict.</li>
<li>20th century: Early in the twentieth century, the Navy awarded many Medals of Honor for peacetime bravery. For instance, in 1901, <a href="/wiki/John_Henry_Helms" title="John Henry Helms">John Henry Helms</a> aboard the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/USS_Chicago_(CA-14)" title="USS Chicago (CA-14)">USS <i>Chicago</i> (CA-14)</a> was awarded the medal for saving the ship's cook from drowning. Seven sailors aboard the <a href="/wiki/USS_Iowa_(BB-4)" title="USS Iowa (BB-4)">USS <i>Iowa</i> (BB-4)</a> were awarded the medal after the ship's boiler exploded on January 25, 1904. Richard Byrd and Floyd Bennett were awarded the medal—combat ("Tiffany") version despite the existence then of a non-combat form of the Navy medal—for the 1926 flight they claim reached the <a href="/wiki/North_Pole" title="North Pole">North Pole</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HargisSinton2003_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-HargisSinton2003-73">[73]</a></sup> And <a href="/wiki/Thomas_J._Ryan_(admiral)" title="Thomas J. Ryan (admiral)">Admiral Thomas J. Ryan</a> was awarded the medal for saving a woman from the burning Grand Hotel in <a href="/wiki/Yokohama" title="Yokohama">Yokohama</a>, Japan, following the 1923 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Kant%C5%8D_earthquake" title="Great Kantō earthquake">Great Kantō earthquake</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ryan_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-ryan-74">[74]</a></sup> Between 1919 and 1942, the Navy issued two separate versions of the Medal of Honor, one for acts related to combat and one for non-combat bravery. The criteria for the award tightened during World War I for the Army version of the Medal of Honor, while the Navy version retained a non-combat provision until 1963. In an Act of Congress of July 9, 1918, the War Department version of the medal required that the recipient "distinguish himself conspicuously by gallantry and intrepidity at the risk of his life above and beyond the call of duty", and also required that the act of valor be performed "in action involving actual conflict with an enemy".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Act_of_July_9.2C_1918.2C_40_Stat._870_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Act_of_July_9.2C_1918.2C_40_Stat._870-75">[75]</a></sup> This was in reaction to the results of the Army Medal of Honor Review Board, which struck 911 medals from the Medal of Honor Roll in February 1917 for lack of basic prerequisites. These included the members of the 27th Maine erroneously awarded the medal for reenlisting to guard the capital during the Civil War, 29 members of Abraham Lincoln's funeral detail, and six civilians, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buffalo_Bill_Cody" title="Buffalo Bill Cody">Buffalo Bill Cody</a> (restored along with four other scouts in 1989)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Mary_Edwards_Walker" title="Mary Edwards Walker">Mary Edwards Walker</a> (though the latter's was restored posthumously in 1977).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup></li>
<li>World War II: Starting in 1942, the Medal would only be awarded for action in combat, although the Navy version of the Medal of Honor technically allowed non-combat awards until 1963.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> Official accounts vary, but generally, the Medal of Honor for combat was known as the "Tiffany Cross", after <a href="/wiki/Tiffany_%26_Co." title="Tiffany &amp; Co.">the company that designed the medal</a>. The Tiffany Cross was first awarded in 1919, but was unpopular partly because of its design.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Tiffany_Cross_Medal_of_Honor" title="Tiffany Cross Medal of Honor">Tiffany Cross Medal of Honor</a> was awarded at least three times for non-combat. By a special authorized <a href="/wiki/Act_of_Congress" title="Act of Congress">Act of Congress</a>, the medal was presented to Byrd and Bennett (see above).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> In 1942, the United States Navy reverted to a single Medal of Honor, although the statute still contained a loophole allowing the award for both "action involving actual conflict with the enemy" or "in the line of his profession".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> Arising from these criteria, approximately 60 percent of the medals earned during and after World War II have been awarded posthumously.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mohstats_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-mohstats-83">[83]</a></sup></li>
<li>Public Law 88-77, July 25, 1963: The requirements for the Medal of Honor were standardized among all the services, requiring that a recipient had "distinguished himself conspicuously by gallantry and intrepidity at the risk of his life above and beyond the call of duty."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> Thus, the act removed the loophole allowing non-combat awards to Navy personnel. The act also clarified that the act of valor must occur during one of three circumstances:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<ol>
<li>While engaged in action against an enemy of the United States</li>
<li>While engaged in military operations involving conflict with an opposing foreign force.</li>
<li>While serving with friendly foreign forces engaged in an armed conflict against an opposing armed force in which the United States is not a belligerent party.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-United_States_Code_1963_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-United_States_Code_1963-86">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup></li>
</ol>
<p>Congress drew the three permutations of combat from President Kennedy's executive order of April 25, 1962, which previously added the same criteria to the <a href="/wiki/Purple_Heart" title="Purple Heart">Purple Heart</a>. On August 24, Kennedy added similar criteria for the Bronze Star Medal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HR2998_1963_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-HR2998_1963-88">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> The amendment was necessary because Cold War armed conflicts did not qualify for consideration under previous statutes such as the 1918 Army Medal of Honor Statute that required valor "in action involving actual conflict with an enemy", since the United States has not formally declared war since World War II as a result of the provisions of the United Nations Charter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> According to congressional testimony by the Army's Deputy Chief of Staff for Personnel, the services were seeking authority to award the Medal of Honor and other valor awards retroactive to July 1, 1958, in areas such as Berlin, Lebanon, Quemoy and Matsu Islands, Taiwan Straits, Congo, Laos, Vietnam, and Cuba.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HR2998_1963_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-HR2998_1963-88">[88]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Authority_and_privileges">Authority and privileges</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Medal_of_Honor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Authority and privileges">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:C-Chatanooga_Cemetery2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="565" data-file-width="660" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/C-Chatanooga_Cemetery2.jpg/220px-C-Chatanooga_Cemetery2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/C-Chatanooga_Cemetery2.jpg/330px-C-Chatanooga_Cemetery2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/C-Chatanooga_Cemetery2.jpg/440px-C-Chatanooga_Cemetery2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>

</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jimmie_W._Monteith_Jr._Gravemarker_03.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1728" data-file-width="2304" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Jimmie_W._Monteith_Jr._Gravemarker_03.jpg/220px-Jimmie_W._Monteith_Jr._Gravemarker_03.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Jimmie_W._Monteith_Jr._Gravemarker_03.jpg/330px-Jimmie_W._Monteith_Jr._Gravemarker_03.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Jimmie_W._Monteith_Jr._Gravemarker_03.jpg/440px-Jimmie_W._Monteith_Jr._Gravemarker_03.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>

</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:James_H_Robinson_grave.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="773" data-file-width="1024" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/James_H_Robinson_grave.jpg/220px-James_H_Robinson_grave.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/James_H_Robinson_grave.jpg/330px-James_H_Robinson_grave.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/James_H_Robinson_grave.jpg/440px-James_H_Robinson_grave.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
