Medal of Honor headstone of <a href="/wiki/James_H._Robinson_(soldier)" title="James H. Robinson (soldier)">James H. Robinson</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Memphis_National_Cemetery" title="Memphis National Cemetery">Memphis National Cemetery</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The four specific authorizing statutes amended July 25, 1963:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-United_States_Code_1963_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-United_States_Code_1963-86">[86]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>Army: <a href="/wiki/Title_10_of_the_United_States_Code" title="Title 10 of the United States Code">10 U.S.C.</a> <a class="external text" href="https://www.law.cornell.edu/uscode/text/10/3741" rel="nofollow">§ 3741</a></li>
<li>Navy and Marine Corps: <a href="/wiki/Title_10_of_the_United_States_Code" title="Title 10 of the United States Code">10 U.S.C.</a> <a class="external text" href="https://www.law.cornell.edu/uscode/text/10/6241" rel="nofollow">§ 6241</a></li>
<li>Air Force: <a href="/wiki/Title_10_of_the_United_States_Code" title="Title 10 of the United States Code">10 U.S.C.</a> <a class="external text" href="https://www.law.cornell.edu/uscode/text/10/8741" rel="nofollow">§ 8741</a></li>
<li>Coast Guard: <a href="/wiki/Title_14_of_the_United_States_Code" title="Title 14 of the United States Code">14 U.S.C.</a> <a class="external text" href="https://www.law.cornell.edu/uscode/text/14/491" rel="nofollow">§ 491</a> A version is authorized but it has never been awarded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[Note 1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-navyfaq_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-navyfaq-43">[43]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<p><i>The President may award, and present in the name of Congress, a medal of honor of appropriate design, with ribbons and appurtenances, to a person who while a member of the Army</i> (naval service; Navy and Marine Corps) (Air Force) (Coast Guard), <i>distinguished himself conspicuously by gallantry and intrepidity at the risk of his life above and beyond the call of duty.</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1998code_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-1998code-92">[91]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Privileges_and_courtesies">Privileges and courtesies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Medal_of_Honor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Privileges and courtesies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Medal of Honor confers special privileges on its recipients. By law, recipients have several benefits:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-benefits_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-benefits-93">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-benefits3_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-benefits3-94">[93]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>Each Medal of Honor recipient may have his or her name entered on the Medal of Honor Roll (<a href="/wiki/Title_38_of_the_United_States_Code" title="Title 38 of the United States Code">38 U.S.C.</a> <a class="external text" href="https://www.law.cornell.edu/uscode/text/38/1560" rel="nofollow">§ 1560</a>).</li>
<li>Each person whose name is placed on the Medal of Honor Roll is certified to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Veterans_Affairs" title="United States Department of Veterans Affairs">United States Department of Veterans Affairs</a> as being entitled to receive a monthly pension above and beyond any military pensions or other benefits for which they may be eligible. The pension is subject to <a href="/wiki/Cost_of_living#Other_uses_of_the_term_.22cost-of-living_allowance.22" title="Cost of living">cost-of-living increases</a>; as of December 1, 2014, it is $1,299.61 a month.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[94]</a></sup></li>
<li>Enlisted recipients of the Medal of Honor are entitled to a supplemental uniform allowance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32USC578.9_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-32USC578.9-96">[95]</a></sup></li>
<li>Recipients receive special entitlements to air transportation under the provisions of DOD Regulation 4515.13-R. This benefit allows the recipient to travel as he or she deems fit across geographical locations, and allows the recipient's dependents to travel either Overseas-Overseas, Overseas-Continental US, or Continental US-Overseas when accompanied by the recipient.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[96]</a></sup></li>
<li>Special identification cards and <a href="/wiki/Defense_Commissary_Agency" title="Defense Commissary Agency">commissary</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Post_exchange" title="Post exchange">exchange</a> privileges are provided for Medal of Honor recipients and their eligible dependents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[97]</a></sup></li>
<li>Recipients are granted eligibility for interment at <a href="/wiki/Arlington_National_Cemetery" title="Arlington National Cemetery">Arlington National Cemetery</a>, if not otherwise eligible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[98]</a></sup></li>
<li>Fully qualified children of recipients are eligible for admission to the <a href="/wiki/List_of_United_States_military_schools_and_academies" title="List of United States military schools and academies">United States military academies</a> without regard to the nomination and quota requirements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[99]</a></sup></li>
<li>Recipients receive a 10 percent increase in retired pay.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[100]</a></sup></li>
<li>Those awarded the medal after October 23, 2002, receive a Medal of Honor Flag. The law specified that all 103 living prior recipients as of that date would receive a flag.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[101]</a></sup></li>
<li>Recipients receive an invitation to all future <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_inauguration" title="United States presidential inauguration">presidential inaugurations</a> and <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_inaugural_balls" title="United States presidential inaugural balls">inaugural balls</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cnnGiunta_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-cnnGiunta-103">[102]</a></sup></li>
<li>As with all medals, retired personnel may wear the Medal of Honor on "appropriate" civilian clothing. Regulations specify that recipients of the Medal of Honor are allowed to wear the uniform "at their pleasure" with standard restrictions on political, commercial, or extremist purposes (other former members of the armed forces may do so only at certain ceremonial occasions).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AR670_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-AR670-104">[103]</a></sup></li>
<li>Most states (40) offer a special license plate for certain types of vehicles to recipients at little or no cost to the recipient.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[104]</a></sup> The states that do not offer Medal of Honor specific license plate offer special license plates for veterans for which recipients may be eligible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[105]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Saluting">Saluting</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Medal_of_Honor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Saluting">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Defense.gov_photo_essay_110713-F-RG147-129.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1996" data-file-width="2994" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Defense.gov_photo_essay_110713-F-RG147-129.jpg/220px-Defense.gov_photo_essay_110713-F-RG147-129.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Defense.gov_photo_essay_110713-F-RG147-129.jpg/330px-Defense.gov_photo_essay_110713-F-RG147-129.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Defense.gov_photo_essay_110713-F-RG147-129.jpg/440px-Defense.gov_photo_essay_110713-F-RG147-129.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Defense.gov_photo_essay_110713-F-RG147-129.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Admiral_(United_States)" title="Admiral (United States)">Admiral</a> <a href="/wiki/Eric_T._Olson" title="Eric T. Olson">Eric T. Olson</a> salutes <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sergeant_First_Class#United_States_Army" title="Sergeant First Class">Sergeant First Class</a> <a href="/wiki/Leroy_Petry" title="Leroy Petry">Leroy Petry</a> at a ceremony at <a href="/wiki/The_Pentagon" title="The Pentagon">The Pentagon</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<ul>
<li>Although not required by law or military regulation,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[106]</a></sup> members of the <a href="/wiki/Uniformed_services_of_the_United_States" title="Uniformed services of the United States">uniformed services</a> are encouraged to render salutes to recipients of the Medal of Honor as a matter of respect and courtesy regardless of rank or status, whether or not they are in uniform.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[107]</a></sup> This is one of the few instances where a living member of the military will receive salutes from members of a higher rank.</li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legal_protection">Legal protection</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Medal_of_Honor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Legal protection">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul>
<li>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Update" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width:52px"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ambox_current_red.svg"><img alt="Ambox current red.svg" data-file-height="290" data-file-width="360" height="34" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Ambox_current_red.svg/42px-Ambox_current_red.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Ambox_current_red.svg/63px-Ambox_current_red.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Ambox_current_red.svg/84px-Ambox_current_red.svg.png 2x" width="42"/></a></div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span">This article needs to be <b>updated</b>. <span class="hide-when-compact">Please update this article to reflect recent events or newly available information.</span> <small><i>(December 2016)</i></small></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
1904: The Army redesigned its Medal of Honor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CMOHS_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-CMOHS-109">[108]</a></sup> To prevent the making of copies of the medal, Brigadier General <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/George_Lewis_Gillespie,_Jr." title="George Lewis Gillespie, Jr.">George Gillespie, Jr.</a>, a Medal of Honor recipient from the Civil War, applied for and obtained a <a href="/wiki/Patent" title="Patent">patent</a> for the new design.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CMOHS_109-1"><a href="#cite_note-CMOHS-109">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Design_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-Design-110">[109]</a></sup> General Gillespie received the patent on November 22, 1904,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Design_110-1"><a href="#cite_note-Design-110">[109]</a></sup> and he transferred it the following month to the Secretary of War at the time, <a href="/wiki/William_Howard_Taft" title="William Howard Taft">William Howard Taft</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CMOHS_109-2"><a href="#cite_note-CMOHS-109">[108]</a></sup></li>
<li>1923: Congress enacted a statute (the year before the 20-year term of the patent would expire)—which would later be codified at 18 U.S.C. §704—prohibiting the unauthorized wearing, manufacturing, or sale of military medals or decorations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[110]</a></sup> In 1994, Congress amended the statute to permit an enhanced penalty if the offense involved the Medal of Honor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[111]</a></sup></li>
<li>2005: Congress enacted the <a href="/wiki/Stolen_Valor_Act_of_2005" title="Stolen Valor Act of 2005">Stolen Valor Act of 2005</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[112]</a></sup> (Section 1 of the Act provided that the law could be cited as the "Stolen Valor Act of 2005", but the bill received final passage and was signed into law in 2006.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[113]</a></sup>) The law amended 18 U.S.C. § 704 to make it a federal criminal offense for a person to deliberately state falsely that he or she had been awarded a military decoration, service medal, or badge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-s1998track_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-s1998track-116">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usc18-704_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-usc18-704-117">[116]</a></sup> The law also permitted an enhanced penalty for someone who falsely claimed to have been awarded the Medal of Honor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usc18-704_117-1"><a href="#cite_note-usc18-704-117">[116]</a></sup></li>
<li>June 28, 2012: In the case of <i><a href="/wiki/United_States_v._Alvarez" title="United States v. Alvarez">United States v. Alvarez</a></i>, the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Supreme Court of the United States">Supreme Court of the United States</a> held that the Stolen Valor Act of 2005's criminalization of the making of false claims of having been awarded a military medal, decoration, or badge was an unconstitutional violation of the <a href="/wiki/First_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="First Amendment to the United States Constitution">First Amendment</a>'s guarantee of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Free_speech" title="Free speech">free speech</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[117]</a></sup> The case involved an elected official in <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a>, Xavier Alvarez, who had falsely stated at a public meeting that he had been awarded the Medal of Honor, even though he had never served in any branch of the armed forces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[118]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<p>The Supreme Court's decision did not specifically address the constitutionality of the older portion of the statute which prohibits the unauthorized wearing, manufacturing, or sale of military medals or decorations. Under the law, the unauthorized wearing, manufacturing, or sale of the Medal of Honor is punishable by a fine of up to $100,000 and imprisonment of up to one year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[119]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>June 3, 2013: President <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a> signs into law a revised version of the Stolen Valor Act, making it a federal offense for someone to pass themselves off as awardees of medals for valor in order to receive benefits or other privileges (such as grants, educational benefits, housing, etc.) that are set aside for veterans and other service members.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[120]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Moh2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="265" data-file-width="400" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Moh2.jpg/220px-Moh2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Moh2.jpg/330px-Moh2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/cc/Moh2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Moh2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
U.S. Army Medal of Honor of <a href="/wiki/Leonard_C._Brostrom" title="Leonard C. Brostrom">Leonard C. Brostrom</a> on display at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latter_Day_Saints" title="Latter Day Saints">Latter Day Saints</a> <a href="/wiki/Church_History_Library" title="Church History Library">Church History Library</a> in downtown <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salt_Lake_City,_Utah" title="Salt Lake City, Utah">Salt Lake City, Utah</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A number of veteran support organizations and private companies devote themselves to exposing those who falsely claim to have received the Medal of Honor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-phony_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-phony-122">[121]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Enforcement">Enforcement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Medal_of_Honor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Enforcement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li>1996: HLI Lordship Industries Inc., a former Medal of Honor contractor, was fined for selling 300 medals for US $75 each.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hli2_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-hli2-123">[122]</a></sup></li>
<li>1996: Fort Lauderdale, Florida, resident Jackie Stern was convicted of wearing a Medal of Honor to which he was not entitled. A federal judge sentenced him to serve one year of probation and to write a letter of apology to each of the then-living 171 recipients of the medal. His letter was published in the local newspaper.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stern_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-stern-124">[123]</a></sup></li>
<li>2003: Edward Fedora and Gisela Fedora were charged with violating <span class="plainlinksneverexpand"><a href="/wiki/Title_18_of_the_United_States_Code" title="Title 18 of the United States Code">18 U.S.C.</a> <a class="external text" href="https://www.law.cornell.edu/uscode/text/18/704#b" rel="nofollow">§ 704(b)</a></span>, <i>Unlawful Sale of a Medal of Honor</i>, for selling medals awarded to U.S. Navy Sailor <a href="/wiki/Robert_Blume" title="Robert Blume">Robert Blume</a> (for action in the <a href="/wiki/Spanish%E2%80%93American_War" title="Spanish–American War">Spanish–American War</a>) and to U.S. Army First Sergeant George Washington Roosevelt (for action in the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">Civil War</a>) to an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/FBI" title="FBI">FBI</a> agent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-blume_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-blume-125">[124]</a></sup> Edward Fedora pleaded guilty and was sentenced to prison.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-edward_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-edward-126">[125]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Duplicate_medals">Duplicate medals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Medal_of_Honor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Duplicate medals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Medal of Honor recipients may apply in writing to the headquarters of the service branch of the medal awarded for a replacement or display Medal of Honor, ribbon, and appurtenance (Medal of Honor flag) without charge. Primary next of kin may also do the same and have any questions answered in regard to the Medal of Honor that was awarded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[126]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Recipients">Recipients</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Medal_of_Honor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Recipients">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Medal_of_Honor_recipients" title="List of Medal of Honor recipients">List of Medal of Honor recipients</a></div>
<p>The Medal of Honor has been awarded to 3,496 different persons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AMOHWStat_4-4"><a href="#cite_note-AMOHWStat-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[127]</a></sup> Of the 19 men who have been awarded the Medal of Honor twice, 14 received two separate medals for two separate actions, while five received both the Navy and Army Medals of Honor for the same action.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[128]</a></sup> As of June 2011, since the beginning of World War II, 851 Medals of Honor have been awarded, 523 (61.45%) posthumously.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CoMH-MOHstatsJune2011_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-CoMH-MOHstatsJune2011-130">[129]</a></sup> One has been awarded to a woman: <a href="/wiki/Mary_Edwards_Walker" title="Mary Edwards Walker">Mary Edwards Walker</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-clinic_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-clinic-131">[130]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>The first Medals of Honor (Army) were awarded by and presented to six "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Andrews_Raiders" title="Andrews Raiders">Andrews Raiders</a>" on March 25, 1863, by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Secretary_of_War" title="Secretary of War">Secretary of War</a> <a href="/wiki/Edwin_Stanton" title="Edwin Stanton">Edwin Stanton</a>, in his office in the War Department. Private <a href="/wiki/Jacob_Parrott" title="Jacob Parrott">Jacob Parrott</a>, a Union Army volunteer from Ohio, became the first recipient of the medal, awarded for his volunteering for and participation in a raid on a <a href="/wiki/Confederate_States_of_America" title="Confederate States of America">Confederate</a> train in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Big_Shanty" title="Big Shanty">Big Shanty</a>, <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(U.S._state)" title="Georgia (U.S. state)">Georgia</a> on April 12, 1862 during the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a>. The six decorated raiders met privately afterward with President Lincoln in his office, in the <a href="/wiki/White_House" title="White House">White House</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MWxviii_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-MWxviii-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[131]</a></sup></li>
<li>The first Medal of Honor (Navy) was awarded by Secretary of War Stanton to 41 sailors on April 4, 1863 (17 for action during the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Forts_Jackson_and_St._Philip" title="Battle of Forts Jackson and St. Philip">Battle of Forts Jackson and St. Philip</a>).</li>
<li>The first Marine awarded the Medal of Honor (Navy) was <a href="/wiki/John_F._Mackie" title="John F. Mackie">John F. Mackie</a> on July 10, 1863, for his rifle action aboard the <a href="/wiki/USS_Galena_(1862)" title="USS Galena (1862)">USS <i>Galena</i></a> on May 15, 1862.</li>
<li>The only Coast Guardsman to be awarded the Medal of Honor (Navy, posthumous) was Signalman First Class <a href="/wiki/Douglas_Albert_Munro" title="Douglas Albert Munro">Douglas Munro</a> on May 27, 1943, for evacuating 500 Marines under fire on September 27, 1942 during the <a href="/wiki/Naval_Battle_of_Guadalcanal" title="Naval Battle of Guadalcanal">Battle of Guadalcanal</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CD19_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-CD19-133">[132]</a></sup> Munro was a Canadian-born, <a href="/wiki/Naturalization#United_States" title="Naturalization">naturalized</a> U.S. citizen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[133]</a></sup></li>
<li>The only woman awarded the Medal of Honor (Army) is <a href="/wiki/Mary_Edwards_Walker" title="Mary Edwards Walker">Mary Edwards Walker</a>, who was a civilian Union Army surgeon during the American Civil War. She received the award in 1865 for the <a href="/wiki/First_Battle_of_Bull_Run" title="First Battle of Bull Run">First Battle of Bull Run</a> (July 21, 1861) and a series of battles to the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Atlanta" title="Battle of Atlanta">Battle of Atlanta</a> in Sept. 1864 ... "for usual medal of honor meritorious services."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-walker_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-walker-135">[134]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<p>The 1917 Medal of Honor Board deleted 911 awards, but only 910 names from the Army Medal of Honor Roll, including awards to Dr. Mary Edwards Walker, <a href="/wiki/Buffalo_Bill" title="Buffalo Bill">William F. "Buffalo Bill" Cody</a> and the first of two awards issued February 10, 1887, to George W. Midil, who retained his award issued October 25, 1893. None of the 910 "deleted" recipients were ordered to return their medals, although on the question of whether the recipients could continue to wear their medals, the Judge Advocate General advised the Medal of Honor Board the Army was not obligated to police the matter. Walker continued to wear her medal until her death. President <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Carter" title="Jimmy Carter">Jimmy Carter</a> formally restored her medal posthumously in 1977.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-clinic_131-1"><a href="#cite_note-clinic-131">[130]</a></sup> As noted above the medals of Buffalo Bill and 4 other scouts were restored in 1989, and even when revoked the award remained in his family's possession.</p>
<ul>
<li>61 <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canadians</a> who served in the United States Armed Forces, mostly during the American Civil War. Since 1900, four Canadians have received the medal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cbc_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-cbc-136">[135]</a></sup> The only Canadian-born, naturalized U.S. citizen to receive the medal for heroism during the Vietnam War was <a href="/wiki/Peter_C._Lemon" title="Peter C. Lemon">Peter C. Lemon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vwam_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-vwam-137">[136]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<p>While the governing statute for the Army Medal of Honor (<a href="/wiki/Title_10_of_the_United_States_Code" title="Title 10 of the United States Code">10 U.S.C.</a> <a class="external text" href="https://www.law.cornell.edu/uscode/text/10/6241" rel="nofollow">§ 6241</a>), beginning in 1918, explicitly stated that a recipient must be "an officer or enlisted man of the Army", "distinguish himself conspicuously by gallantry and intrepidity at the risk of his life above and beyond the call of duty", and perform an act of valor "in action involving actual conflict with an enemy",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Act_of_July_9.2C_1918.2C_40_Stat._870_75-1"><a href="#cite_note-Act_of_July_9.2C_1918.2C_40_Stat._870-75">[75]</a></sup> exceptions have been made:</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Charles_Lindbergh" title="Charles Lindbergh">Charles Lindbergh</a>, 1927, civilian pilot, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Army_Air_Corps" title="U.S. Army Air Corps">U.S. Army Air Corps</a> reserve officer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[137]</a></sup> Lindbergh's medal was authorized by a special act of Congress that directly contradicted the July 1918 act of Congress that required that all Army recipients be "in action involving actual conflict with an enemy".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Act_of_July_9.2C_1918.2C_40_Stat._870_75-2"><a href="#cite_note-Act_of_July_9.2C_1918.2C_40_Stat._870-75">[75]</a></sup> The award was based on the previous acts authorizing the Navy medal to Byrd and Bennett (see above). Some congressmen objected to Lindbergh's award because it contradicted the 1918 statute, but Representative Snell reportedly quelled this dissent by explaining that "it was and it wasn't the Congressional Medal of Honor which Lindbergh would receive under his bill; that the Lindbergh medal would be entirely distinct from the valor award for war service."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[138]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Kenneth_M._Taylor" title="Kenneth M. Taylor">Kenneth M. Taylor</a> and <a href="/wiki/George_Welch_(pilot)" title="George Welch (pilot)">George Welch</a> were nominated but not awarded because they acted without orders.</li>
<li>Major General (Retired) <a href="/wiki/Adolphus_Greely" title="Adolphus Greely">Adolphus Greely</a> was awarded the medal in 1935, on his 91st birthday, "for his life of splendid public service". The result of a special act of Congress similar to Lindbergh's, Greely's medal citation did not reference any acts of valor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[139]</a></sup></li>
<li>Foreign unknown recipients include the <a href="/wiki/The_Unknown_Warrior" title="The Unknown Warrior">British Unknown Warrior</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Arc_de_Triomphe#The_Unknown_Soldier" title="Arc de Triomphe">French Unknown Soldier</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tomb_of_the_Unknown_Soldier_in_Bucharest" title="Tomb of the Unknown Soldier in Bucharest">Romanian Unknown Soldier</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monument_of_Vittorio_Emanuele_II#Unknown_soldier" title="Monument of Vittorio Emanuele II">Italian Unknown Soldier</a>, and the Belgian Unknown Soldier.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[140]</a></sup></li>
<li>U.S. unknown recipients include the Unknowns of World War I,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[141]</a></sup> World War II,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[142]</a></sup> Korea,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[143]</a></sup> and Vietnam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[144]</a></sup> The Vietnam Unknown was later identified as Air Force 1st <a href="/wiki/Michael_Blassie" title="Michael Blassie">Lt. Michael J. Blassie</a> through the use of DNA identification. Blassie's family asked for his Medal of Honor, but the Department of Defense denied the request in 1998. According to Undersecretary of Defense Rudy de Leon, the medal was awarded symbolically to all Vietnam unknowns, not to Blassie specifically.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[145]</a></sup></li>
</ul>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Double_recipients">Double recipients</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Medal_of_Honor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Double recipients">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Nineteen men have been awarded the Medal of Honor twice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[146]</a></sup> The first two-time Medal of Honor recipient was <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Custer" title="Thomas Custer">Thomas Custer</a> (brother of <a href="/wiki/George_Armstrong_Custer" title="George Armstrong Custer">George Armstrong Custer</a>) for two separate actions that took place several days apart during the American Civil War.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[147]</a></sup></p>
<p>Five "double recipients" were awarded both the Army and Navy Medal of Honor for the same action; all five of these occurrences took place during World War I.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[148]</a></sup> Since February 1919, no single individual can be awarded more than one Medal of Honor for the same action, although a member of one branch of the armed forces can receive the Medal of Honor from another branch if the actions for which it was awarded occurred under the authority of the second branch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[149]</a></sup></p>
<p>To date, the maximum number of Medals of Honor earned by any service member has been two.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-navyfaq_43-2"><a href="#cite_note-navyfaq-43">[43]</a></sup> The last individual to be awarded two Medals of Honor was <a href="/wiki/John_J._Kelly" title="John J. Kelly">John J. Kelly</a> in 1918; the last individual to receive two Medals of Honor for two different actions was <a href="/wiki/Smedley_Butler" title="Smedley Butler">Smedley Butler</a>, in 1914 and 1915.</p>
<dl>
<dd><small>§ Rank refers to rank held at time of Medal of Honor action.</small></dd>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Related_recipients">Related recipients</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Medal_of_Honor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Related recipients">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arthur_MacArthur,_Jr." title="Arthur MacArthur, Jr.">Arthur MacArthur, Jr.</a> and <a href="/wiki/Douglas_MacArthur" title="Douglas MacArthur">Douglas MacArthur</a> are the first father and son to be awarded the Medal of Honor. The only other such pairing is <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Roosevelt" title="Theodore Roosevelt">Theodore Roosevelt</a> (awarded in 2001) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theodore_Roosevelt,_Jr." title="Theodore Roosevelt, Jr.">Theodore Roosevelt, Jr.</a></p>
<p>Five pairs of brothers have received the Medal of Honor:</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/John_C._Black" title="John C. Black">John</a> and <a href="/wiki/William_P._Black" title="William P. Black">William Black</a>, in the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a>. The Blacks are the first brothers to be so honored.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Charles_E._Capehart" title="Charles E. Capehart">Charles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Henry_Capehart" title="Henry Capehart">Henry Capehart</a>, in the American Civil War, the latter for saving a drowning man while under fire.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Antoine_August_Michel_Gaujot" title="Antoine August Michel Gaujot">Antoine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Julien_Edmund_Victor_Gaujot" title="Julien Edmund Victor Gaujot">Julien Gaujot</a>. The Gaujots also have the unique distinction of receiving their medals for actions in separate conflicts, Antoine in the <a href="/wiki/Philippine%E2%80%93American_War" title="Philippine–American War">Philippine–American War</a> and Julien when he crossed the border to rescue Mexicans and Americans in a <a href="/wiki/Mexican_Revolution" title="Mexican Revolution">Mexican Revolution</a> skirmish.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Harry_Herbert_Miller" title="Harry Herbert Miller">Harry</a> and <a href="/wiki/Willard_Miller" title="Willard Miller">Willard Miller</a>, during the same naval action in the <a href="/wiki/Spanish%E2%80%93American_War" title="Spanish–American War">Spanish–American War</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Allen_and_James_Thompson" title="Allen and James Thompson">Allen and James Thompson</a>, in the same American Civil War action.</li>
</ul>
<p>Another notable pair of related recipients are Admiral <a href="/wiki/Frank_Friday_Fletcher" title="Frank Friday Fletcher">Frank Friday Fletcher</a> (rear admiral at the time of award) and his nephew, Admiral <a href="/wiki/Frank_Jack_Fletcher" title="Frank Jack Fletcher">Frank Jack Fletcher</a> (lieutenant at the time of award), both awarded for actions during the <a href="/wiki/United_States_occupation_of_Veracruz" title="United States occupation of Veracruz">United States occupation of Veracruz</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Belated_recognition">Belated recognition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Medal_of_Honor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Belated recognition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Since 1979, 85 belated Medal of Honor decorations were presented to recognize actions from the Civil War to the Vietnam War. In addition, five recipients whose names were not included on the Army Medal of Honor Roll in 1917 had their awards restored.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[151]</a></sup></p>
<p>A 1993 study commissioned by the U.S. Army investigated "racial disparity" in the awarding of medals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-African_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-African-153">[152]</a></sup> At the time, no Medals of Honor had been awarded to American soldiers of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/African_descent" title="African descent">African descent</a> who served in World War II. After an exhaustive review, the study recommended that ten <a href="/wiki/Distinguished_Service_Cross_(United_States)" title="Distinguished Service Cross (United States)">Distinguished Service Cross</a> recipients be awarded the Medal of Honor. On January 13, 1997, President <a href="/wiki/Bill_Clinton" title="Bill Clinton">Bill Clinton</a> presented the Medal of Honor to seven of these World War II veterans, six of them posthumously and one to former Second Lieutenant <a href="/wiki/Vernon_Baker" title="Vernon Baker">Vernon Baker</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CD25_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-CD25-154">[153]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1998, a similar study of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asian_American" title="Asian American">Asian Americans</a> resulted in President <a href="/wiki/Bill_Clinton" title="Bill Clinton">Bill Clinton</a> presenting 22 Medals of Honor in 2000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AsianDoD_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-AsianDoD-155">[154]</a></sup> Twenty of these medals went to American soldiers of <a href="/wiki/Japanese_people" title="Japanese people">Japanese</a> descent of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/442nd_Regimental_Combat_Team" title="442nd Regimental Combat Team">442nd Regimental Combat Team</a> (442nd RCT) who served in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_Theater_of_Operations" title="European Theater of Operations">European Theater of Operations</a> during World War II.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AsianDoD_155-1"><a href="#cite_note-AsianDoD-155">[154]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[155]</a></sup> One of these Medal of Honor recipients was Senator <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Inouye" title="Daniel Inouye">Daniel Inouye</a>, a former U.S. Army officer in the 442nd RCT.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CD25_154-1"><a href="#cite_note-CD25-154">[153]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2005, President <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a> presented the Medal of Honor to <a href="/wiki/Tibor_Rubin" title="Tibor Rubin">Tibor Rubin</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Hungary" title="Hungary">Hungarian</a>-born <a href="/wiki/American_Jews" title="American Jews">American Jew</a> who was a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Holocaust_survivor" title="Holocaust survivor">Holocaust survivor</a> of World War II and enlisted U.S. infantryman and <a href="/wiki/Prisoner_of_war" title="Prisoner of war">prisoner of war</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Korean_War" title="Korean War">Korean War</a>, whom many believed to have been overlooked because of his <a href="/wiki/Religion" title="Religion">religion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[156]</a></sup></p>
<p>On April 11, 2013, President Obama presented the Medal of Honor posthumously to <a href="/wiki/Chaplain_Corps_(United_States_Army)" title="Chaplain Corps (United States Army)">Army chaplain</a> Captain <a href="/wiki/Emil_Kapaun" title="Emil Kapaun">Emil Kapaun</a> for his actions as a <a href="/wiki/Korean_War#Prisoners_of_war" title="Korean War">prisoner of war</a> during the Korean War.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[157]</a></sup> This follows other awards to Army Sergeant <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leslie_H._Sabo,_Jr." title="Leslie H. Sabo, Jr.">Leslie H. Sabo, Jr.</a> for conspicuous gallantry in action on May 10, 1970, near <a href="/wiki/Tonl%C3%A9_San" title="Tonlé San">Se San</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cambodia" title="Cambodia">Cambodia</a>, during the Vietnam War<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cbsnews5-16-12_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-cbsnews5-16-12-159">[158]</a></sup> and to Army Private First Class <a href="/wiki/Henry_Svehla" title="Henry Svehla">Henry Svehla</a> and Army Private First Class <a href="/wiki/Anthony_T._Kaho%CA%BBohanohano" title="Anthony T. Kahoʻohanohano">Anthony T. Kahoʻohanohano</a> for their heroic actions during the Korean War.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[159]</a></sup></p>
<p>As a result of a Congressionally mandated review to ensure brave acts were not overlooked due to prejudice or discrimination, on March 18, 2014 President Obama upgraded <a href="/wiki/Distinguished_Service_Cross_(United_States)" title="Distinguished Service Cross (United States)">Distinguished Service Crosses</a> to Medals of Honor for 24 Hispanic, Jewish, and African American individuals—the "Valor 24"—for their actions in World War II, the Korean War and the Vietnam War.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USarmyCAC201403_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-USarmyCAC201403-161">[160]</a></sup> Three were still living at the time of the ceremony.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USarmyCAC201403_161-1"><a href="#cite_note-USarmyCAC201403-161">[160]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="27th_Maine_and_other_revoked_awardings">27th Maine and other revoked awardings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Medal_of_Honor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: 27th Maine and other revoked awardings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Medal_of_Honor_monument,_Austin,_TX_IMG_2206.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3240" data-file-width="4320" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Medal_of_Honor_monument%2C_Austin%2C_TX_IMG_2206.JPG/220px-Medal_of_Honor_monument%2C_Austin%2C_TX_IMG_2206.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Medal_of_Honor_monument%2C_Austin%2C_TX_IMG_2206.JPG/330px-Medal_of_Honor_monument%2C_Austin%2C_TX_IMG_2206.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Medal_of_Honor_monument%2C_Austin%2C_TX_IMG_2206.JPG/440px-Medal_of_Honor_monument%2C_Austin%2C_TX_IMG_2206.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Medal_of_Honor_monument,_Austin,_TX_IMG_2206.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A Medal of Honor monument at the <a href="/wiki/Texas_State_Cemetery" title="Texas State Cemetery">Texas State Cemetery</a> in <a href="/wiki/Austin,_Texas" title="Austin, Texas">Austin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">Texas</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During the Civil War, <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_War" title="United States Secretary of War">Secretary of War</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edwin_M._Stanton" title="Edwin M. Stanton">Edwin M. Stanton</a> promised a Medal of Honor to every man in the <a href="/wiki/27th_Maine_Volunteer_Infantry_Regiment" title="27th Maine Volunteer Infantry Regiment">27th Maine Volunteer Infantry Regiment</a> who extended his enlistment beyond the agreed-upon date. The Battle of Gettysburg was imminent, and 311 men of the regiment volunteered to serve until the battle was resolved. The remaining men returned to Maine, but with the Union victory at Gettysburg the 311 volunteers soon followed. The volunteers arrived back in Maine in time to be discharged with the men who had earlier returned. Since there seemed to be no official list of the 311 volunteers, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_War" title="United States Department of War">War Department</a> exacerbated the situation by forwarding 864 medals to the commanding officer of the regiment. The commanding officer only issued the medals to the volunteers who stayed behind and retained the others on the grounds that, if he returned the remainder to the War Department, the War Department would try to reissue the medals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MWxix_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-MWxix-162">[161]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1916, a board of five Army generals on the retired list convened under act of law to review every Army Medal of Honor awarded. The board was to report on any Medals of Honor awarded or issued for any cause other than distinguished service. The commission, led by <a href="/wiki/Nelson_A._Miles" title="Nelson A. Miles">Nelson A. Miles</a>, identified 911 awards for causes other than distinguished service. This included the 864 medals awarded to members of the 27th Maine regiment; 29 servicemen who served as Abraham Lincoln's funeral guard; six civilians, including Mary Edwards Walker and Buffalo Bill Cody; and 12 others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MWxxv_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-MWxxv-163">[162]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CD15_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-CD15-164">[163]</a></sup> Walker's medal was restored by President <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Carter" title="Jimmy Carter">Jimmy Carter</a> in 1977.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-walker_135-1"><a href="#cite_note-walker-135">[134]</a></sup> Cody and four other civilian scouts who rendered distinguished service in action, and who were therefore considered by the board to have fully earned their medals, had theirs restored in 1989.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CD16_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-CD16-165">[164]</a></sup> The report was endorsed by the <a href="/wiki/Judge_Advocate_General%27s_Corps" title="Judge Advocate General's Corps">Judge Advocate General</a>, who also advised that the War Department should not seek the return of the revoked medals from the recipients identified by the board. In the case of recipients who continued to wear the medal, the War Department was advised to take no action to enforce the statute.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[165]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Nomination_under_current_consideration">Nomination under current consideration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Medal_of_Honor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Nomination under current consideration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><a href="/wiki/David_Dunnels_White" title="David Dunnels White">David Dunnels White</a>'s nomination is under consideration for the capture of Confederate Major General <a href="/wiki/George_Washington_Custis_Lee" title="George Washington Custis Lee">G. W. Custis Lee</a>, eldest son of General <a href="/wiki/Robert_E._Lee" title="Robert E. Lee">Robert E. Lee</a>, at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Sailor%27s_Creek" title="Battle of Sailor's Creek">Battle of Sailor's Creek</a>, Virginia, on April 6, 1865.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Similar_American_decorations">Similar American decorations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Medal_of_Honor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Similar American decorations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The following decorations, in one degree or another, bear similar names to the Medal of Honor, but are entirely separate awards with different criteria for issuance:</p>
<ul>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cardenas_Medal_of_Honor" title="Cardenas Medal of Honor">Cardenas Medal of Honor</a>: decoration of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Revenue_Cutter_Service" title="United States Revenue Cutter Service">United States Revenue Cutter Service</a>, which was later merged into the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Coast_Guard" title="United States Coast Guard">United States Coast Guard</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chaplain%27s_Medal_for_Heroism" title="Chaplain's Medal for Heroism">Chaplain's Medal for Heroism</a>: awarded posthumously for a single action to <a href="/wiki/Four_Chaplains" title="Four Chaplains">four recipients</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Congressional_Gold_Medal" title="Congressional Gold Medal">Congressional Gold Medal</a>: the highest civilian honor bestowed by the United States (along with the Presidential Medal of Freedom)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Congressional_Space_Medal_of_Honor" title="Congressional Space Medal of Honor">Congressional Space Medal of Honor</a>: intended for issuance to astronauts, but despite its name, it is not equal to the Medal of Honor</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Presidential_Medal_of_Freedom" title="Presidential Medal of Freedom">Presidential Medal of Freedom</a>: the highest civilian honor bestowed by the United States (along with the Congressional Gold Medal)</li>
<li>Several <a href="/wiki/United_States_law_enforcement_decorations" title="United States law enforcement decorations">United States law enforcement decorations</a> bear the name "Medal of Honor". The <a href="/wiki/Public_Safety_Officer_Medal_of_Valor" title="Public Safety Officer Medal of Valor">Public Safety Officer Medal of Valor</a>, established by Congress in 2001 and stated to be "the highest National award for valor by a public safety officer", is also awarded by the President of the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[166]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
